<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Nuclear power</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Power generated from nuclear reactions</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Atomic power" redirects here. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Power_(film)" title="Atomic Power (film)">Atomic Power (film)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For countries with the power or ability to project nuclear weapons, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">List of states with nuclear weapons</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg/220px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg/330px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg/440px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Leibstadt_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant">Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant</a> in Switzerland</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_generation.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Nuclear_power_generation.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_generation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Nuclear_power_generation.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_generation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Nuclear_power_generation.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_generation.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_generation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Growth of worldwide nuclear power generation</div></div></div>
<p><b>Nuclear power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. Nuclear power can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions. Presently, the vast majority of electricity from nuclear power is produced by nuclear <i>fission</i> of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a>. Nuclear <i>decay</i> processes are used in niche applications such as <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> in some space probes such as <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>. Generating electricity from <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power"><i>fusion</i> power</a> remains the focus of international research.
</p><p>Most nuclear power plants use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_reactor" title="Thermal reactor">thermal reactors</a> with <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle#Once-through_nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">once-through fuel cycle</a>. Fuel is removed when the percentage of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_poison" title="Neutron poison">neutron absorbing atoms</a> becomes so large that a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">chain reaction</a> can no longer be sustained, typically three years. It is then cooled for several years in on-site <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pools</a> before being transferred to long term storage. The spent fuel, though low in volume, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste" title="High-level radioactive waste">high-level radioactive waste</a>. While its radioactivity decreases exponentially it must be isolated from the biosphere for hundreds of thousands of years, though newer technologies (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactors</a>) have the potential to reduce this significantly. Because the spent fuel is still mostly fissionable material, some countries (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_France" title="Nuclear power in France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Russia" title="Nuclear power in Russia">Russia</a>) <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocess</a> their spent fuel by extracting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">fertile</a> elements for fabrication in new fuel, although this process is more expensive than producing new fuel from <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">mined uranium</a>. All reactors breed some <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, which is found in the spent fuel, and because Pu-239 is the preferred material for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a>, reprocessing is seen as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">weapon proliferation</a> risk.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_nuclear_power_plant" title="First nuclear power plant">first nuclear power plant</a> was built in the 1950s. The global installed nuclear capacity grew to 100<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW in the late 1970s, and then expanded rapidly during the 1980s, reaching 300<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW by 1990. The 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in the United States and the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> in the Soviet Union resulted in increased regulation and public opposition to nuclear plants.  These factors, along with high cost of construction, resulted in the global installed capacity only increasing to 390<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW by 2022.  These plants supplied 2,586 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) of electricity in 2019, equivalent to about 10% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_electricity_generation" title="Global electricity generation">global electricity generation</a>, and were the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> source after <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>. As of March 2022,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">439 civilian fission reactors in the world</a>, with overall capacity of 393<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup> 56 under construction and 96 planned, with a combined capacity of 62<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW and 96<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW, respectively. The United States has the largest fleet of nuclear reactors, generating over 800<span class="nowrap"> </span>TWh of zero-emissions electricity per year with an average <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> of 92%. Average global capacity factor is 89%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup> Most new reactors under construction are <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactors</a> in Asia.
</p><p>Nuclear power generation causes one of the lowest levels of fatalities per unit of energy generated compared to other energy sources. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and hydroelectricity each have caused more fatalities per unit of energy due to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">accidents</a>. Nuclear power plants emit no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>. One of the dangers of nuclear power is the potential for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">accidents</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a> in Japan in 2011.
</p><p>There is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">debate about nuclear power</a>. Proponents contend that nuclear power is a safe, sustainable energy source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">anti-nuclear movement</a> contends that nuclear power poses many threats to people and the environment and is too expensive and slow to deploy when compared to alternative <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> sources.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_nuclear_power" title="History of nuclear power">History of nuclear power</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/220px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/330px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/440px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first light bulbs ever lit by electricity generated by nuclear power at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-1" title="EBR-1">EBR-1</a> at <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>-West, December 20, 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The discovery of nuclear fission occurred in 1938 following over four decades of work on the science of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> and the elaboration of new <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear physics</a> that described the components of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>.
Soon after the discovery of the fission process, it was realized that a fissioning nucleus can induce further nucleus fissions, thus inducing a self-sustaining chain reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office-3">[3]</a></sup>
Once this was experimentally confirmed in 1939, scientists in many countries petitioned their governments for support of nuclear fission research, just on the cusp of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, for the development of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, these research efforts led to the creation of the first man-made nuclear reactor, the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, which achieved <a href="/wiki/Criticality_(status)" title="Criticality (status)">criticality</a> on December 2, 1942. The reactor's development was part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> effort to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bombs" title="Atomic bombs">atomic bombs</a> during World War II. It led to the building of larger single-purpose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Production_reactor" title="Production reactor">production reactors</a> for the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons-grade_plutonium" title="Weapons-grade plutonium">weapons-grade plutonium</a> for use in the first nuclear weapons. The United States tested the first nuclear weapon in July 1945, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity test</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> taking place one month later.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nautiluscore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="386" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg/220px-Nautiluscore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg/330px-Nautiluscore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nautiluscore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The launching ceremony of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS <i>Nautilus</i></a> January 1954. In 1958 it would become the first vessel to reach the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2558" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/220px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/330px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/440px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall nuclear power station</a> in the United Kingdom, the world's first commercial nuclear power station.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the military nature of the first nuclear devices, the 1940s and 1950s were characterized by strong optimism for the potential of nuclear power to provide cheap and endless energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
Electricity was generated for the first time by a nuclear reactor on December 20, 1951, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-I" title="EBR-I">EBR-I</a> experimental station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, which initially produced about 100<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KW" title="KW">kW</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
In 1953, American President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> gave his "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>" speech at the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, emphasizing the need to develop "peaceful" uses of nuclear power quickly. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1954" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1954">Atomic Energy Act of 1954</a> which allowed rapid declassification of U.S. reactor technology and encouraged development by the private sector.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_power_generation">First power generation</span></h3>
<p>The first organization to develop practical nuclear power was the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/S1W_reactor" title="S1W reactor">S1W reactor</a> for the purpose of propelling <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. The first nuclear-powered submarine, <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS <i>Nautilus</i></a>, was put to sea in January 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
The S1W reactor was a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a>. This design was chosen because it was simpler, more compact, and easier to operate compared to alternative designs, thus more suitable to be used in submarines. This decision would result in the PWR being the reactor of choice also for power generation, thus having a lasting impact on the civilian electricity market in the years to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 27, 1954, the <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a> became the world's first nuclear power plant to generate electricity for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, producing around 5 megawatts of electric power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEANews_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEANews-12">[12]</a></sup>
The world's first commercial nuclear power station, <a href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall</a> at Windscale, England was connected to the national power grid on 27 August 1956. In common with a number of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_I_reactor" title="Generation I reactor">generation I reactors</a>, the plant had the dual purpose of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, the latter for the nascent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear weapons program in Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_first_opposition">Expansion and first opposition</span></h3>
<p>The total global installed nuclear capacity initially rose relatively quickly, rising from less than 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW) in 1960 to 100<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW in the late 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-9">[9]</a></sup>
During the 1970s and 1980s rising economic costs (related to extended construction times largely due to regulatory changes and pressure-group litigation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernard_L._Cohen_1990_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_L._Cohen_1990-14">[14]</a></sup> and falling fossil fuel prices made nuclear power plants then under construction less attractive. In the 1980s in the U.S. and 1990s in Europe, the flat electric grid growth and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_liberalization" title="Electricity liberalization">electricity liberalization</a> also made the addition of large new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy generators economically unattractive.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> had a significant effect on countries, such as <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, which had relied more heavily on oil for electric generation to invest in nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
France would construct 25 nuclear power plants over the next 15 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palfreman_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-palfreman-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_preneuf_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_preneuf-17">[17]</a></sup> and as of 2019, 71% of French electricity was generated by nuclear power, the highest percentage by any nation in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some local opposition to nuclear power emerged in the United States in the early 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-well_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-well-19">[19]</a></sup> In the late 1960s some members of the scientific community began to express pointed concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-20">[20]</a></sup> These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> concerns related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" title="Nuclear accidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">radioactive waste disposal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bm_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-bm-21">[21]</a></sup>
In the early 1970s, there were large protests about a proposed nuclear power plant in <a href="/wiki/Wyhl" title="Wyhl">Wyhl</a>, Germany. The project was cancelled in 1975. The anti-nuclear success at Wyhl inspired opposition to nuclear power in other parts of Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pub_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pub-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-got_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-got-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-1970s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> activism gained a wider appeal and influence, and nuclear power began to become an issue of major public protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jimfalk_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-jimfalk-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-25">[25]</a></sup>
In some countries, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">nuclear power conflict</a> "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-kits-27">[27]</a></sup> The increased public hostility to nuclear power led to a longer license procurement process, regulations and increased requirements for safety equipment, which made new construction much more expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phyast.pitt.edu_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-phyast.pitt.edu-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
In the United States, over <a href="/wiki/List_of_cancelled_nuclear_reactors_in_the_United_States" title="List of cancelled nuclear reactors in the United States">120 LWR reactor proposals were ultimately cancelled</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and the construction of new reactors ground to a halt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-31">[31]</a></sup>
The 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">accident at Three Mile Island</a> with no fatalities, played a major part in the reduction in the number of new plant constructions in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chernobyl_and_renaissance">Chernobyl and renaissance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2167" data-file-width="3852" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/220px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/330px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/440px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pripyat_(city)" title="Pripyat (city)">Pripyat</a> abandoned since 1986, with the Chernobyl plant and the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_New_Safe_Confinement" title="Chernobyl New Safe Confinement">Chernobyl New Safe Confinement</a> arch in the distance</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2813" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/220px-OL3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/330px-OL3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/440px-OL3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_3" title="Olkiluoto 3">Olkiluoto 3</a> under construction in 2009. It was the first <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR</a>, a modernized PWR design, to start construction.</div></div></div>
<p>During the 1980s one new nuclear reactor started up every 17 days on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> By the end of the decade, global installed nuclear capacity reached 300<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW. Since the late 1980s, new capacity additions slowed down significantly, with the installed nuclear capacity reaching 366<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW in 2005.
</p><p>The 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>, involving an <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> reactor, altered the development of nuclear power and led to a greater focus on meeting international safety and regulatory standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
It is considered the worst nuclear disaster in history both in total casualties, with 56 direct deaths, and financially, with the cleanup and the cost estimated at 18<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_rouble" title="Soviet rouble">Rbls</a> (US$68<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion in 2019, adjusted for inflation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GorbachevBoC_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-GorbachevBoC-35">[35]</a></sup> The international organization to promote safety awareness and the professional development of operators in nuclear facilities, the <a href="/wiki/World_Association_of_Nuclear_Operators" title="World Association of Nuclear Operators">World Association of Nuclear Operators</a> (WANO), was created as a direct outcome of the 1986 Chernobyl accident.
The Chernobyl disaster played a major part in the reduction in the number of new plant constructions in the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-20">[20]</a></sup> Influenced by these events, Italy voted against nuclear power in a 1987 referendum, becoming the first country to completely phase out nuclear power in 1990.
</p><p>In the early 2000s, nuclear energy was expecting a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">nuclear renaissance</a>, an increase in the construction of new reactors, due to concerns about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="Carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36">[36]</a></sup>
During this period, newer <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactors</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR</a> began construction.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fukushima">Fukushima</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:522px"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:520px;">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1039153519">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mw-graph{min-width:100%!important;max-width:100%;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:visible}}.mw-parser-output .mw-graph-img{width:inherit;height:inherit}</style><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="761005a6474093336b8e19c9ba6fffed33cdf01d" style="min-width:180px;min-height:150px"></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1039153519" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="9592929825135af92c29bdcbf7ce3c534580b9e6" style="min-width:180px;min-height:150px"></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Nuclear power generation (TWh) and operational nuclear reactors since 1997<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pris-supplied_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pris-supplied-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Prospects of a nuclear renaissance were delayed by another nuclear accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbonbrief_2016_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-carbonbrief_2016-38">[38]</a></sup>
The 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_accident" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident</a> was caused by a large tsunami triggered by the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">Tōhoku earthquake</a>, one of the largest earthquakes ever recorded. The <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a> suffered three core meltdowns due to failure of the emergency cooling system for lack of electricity supply. This resulted in the most serious nuclear accident since the Chernobyl disaster. The accident prompted a re-examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">nuclear energy policy</a> in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-39">[39]</a></sup>
Germany approved plans to close all its reactors by 2022, and many other countries reviewed their nuclear power programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.xinhuanet.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.xinhuanet.com-43">[43]</a></sup>
Following the disaster, Japan shut down all of its nuclear power reactors, some of them permanently, and in 2015 began a gradual process to restart the remaining 40 reactors, following safety checks and based on revised criteria for operations and public approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_prospects">Current prospects</span></h3>
<p>By 2015, the IAEA's outlook for nuclear energy had become more promising, recognizing the importance of low-carbon generation for mitigating climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the global trend was for new nuclear power stations coming online to be balanced by the number of old plants being retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
In 2016, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Energy_Information_Administration" title="U.S. Energy Information Administration">U.S. Energy Information Administration</a> projected for its "base case" that world nuclear power generation would increase from 2,344 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) in 2012 to 4,500<span class="nowrap"> </span>TWh in 2040. Most of the predicted increase was expected to be in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> As of 2018, there are over 150 nuclear reactors planned including 50 under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In January 2019, China had 45 reactors in operation, 13 under construction, and plans to build 43 more, which would make it the world's largest generator of nuclear electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-china19_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-china19-49">[49]</a></sup> As of 2021, 17 reactors were reported to be under construction. China built significantly fewer reactors than originally planned, its share of electricity from nuclear power was 5% in 2019<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dwfrance_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-dwfrance-50">[50]</a></sup> and observers have cautioned that, along with the risks, the changing economics of energy generation may cause new nuclear energy plants to "no longer make sense in a world that is leaning toward cheaper, more reliable renewable energy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnchina_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnchina-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-francere_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-francere-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2021, the Japanese cabinet approved the new Plan for Electricity Generation to 2030 prepared by the Agency for Natural Resources and Energy (ANRE) and an advisory committee, following public consultation. The nuclear target for 2030 requires the restart of another ten reactors. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Fumio_Kishida" title="Fumio Kishida">Fumio Kishida</a> in July 2022 announced that the country should consider building advanced reactors and extending operating licences beyond 60 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2022, with world oil and gas prices on the rise, while Germany is restarting its coal plants to deal with loss of Russian gas that it needs to supplement its Energiwende,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> many other countries have announced ambitious plans to reinvigorate ageing nuclear generating capacity with new investments. French President <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> announced his intention to build six new reactors in coming decades, placing nuclear at the heart of France's drive for carbon neutrality by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_plants">Power plants</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="64" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm" height="124" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm/220px-seek%3D2-PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="492720" data-framerate="30" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e3/PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm/PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
