<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:220px;">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1039153519" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="5a38e15f8767befe8c5f48ff73c81b4a8e5e7f1a" style="min-width:100px;min-height:100px"></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Number of electricity-generating civilian reactors by type as of 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_reactors_stats_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_reactors_stats-56">[56]</a></sup>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 4em;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#1f77b4; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">PWR</a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff7f0e; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">BWR</a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#2ca02c; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Gas-cooled_reactor" title="Gas-cooled reactor">GCR</a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d62728; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_heavy-water_reactor" title="Pressurized heavy-water reactor">PHWR</a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#9467bd; color:black;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_graphite-moderated_reactor" title="Light water graphite-moderated reactor">LWGR</a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#8c564b; color:white;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_breeder_reactor" title="Fast breeder reactor">FBR</a></span></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">Nuclear reactor</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_commercial_nuclear_reactors" title="List of commercial nuclear reactors">List of commercial nuclear reactors</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_power_stations" title="List of nuclear power stations">List of nuclear power stations</a></div>
<p>Nuclear power plants are <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a> that generate electricity by harnessing the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> released from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>.
A fission nuclear power plant is generally composed of: a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, in which the nuclear reactions generating heat take place; a cooling system, which removes the heat from inside the reactor; a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>, which transforms the heat into <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical energy</a>; an <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a>, which transforms the mechanical energy into electrical energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>When a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> hits the nucleus of a <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> atom, it can split the nucleus into two smaller nuclei, which is a nuclear fission reaction. The reaction releases energy and neutrons. The released neutrons can hit other uranium or plutonium nuclei, causing new fission reactions, which release more energy and more neutrons. This is called a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">chain reaction</a>.
In most commercial reactors, the reaction rate is contained by <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> that absorb excess neutrons.
The controllability of nuclear reactors depends on the fact that a small fraction of neutrons resulting from fission are <a href="/wiki/Delayed_neutron" title="Delayed neutron">delayed</a>. The time delay between the fission and the release of the neutrons slows down changes in reaction rates and gives time for moving the control rods to adjust the reaction rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_cycle">Fuel cycle</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="923" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/220px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/330px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/440px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nuclear fuel cycle begins when uranium is mined, enriched, and manufactured into nuclear fuel (1), which is delivered to a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. After use, the spent fuel is delivered to a reprocessing plant (2) or to a final repository (3). In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">nuclear reprocessing</a> 95% of spent fuel can potentially be recycled to be returned to use in a power plant (4).</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle_Information_System" title="Integrated Nuclear Fuel Cycle Information System">Integrated Nuclear Fuel Cycle Information System</a></div>
<p>The life cycle of nuclear fuel starts with <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">uranium mining</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ore</a> is then converted into a compact <a href="/wiki/Ore_concentrate" title="Ore concentrate">ore concentrate</a> form, known as <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a> (U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>), to facilitate transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc_fuel_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_fuel-59">[59]</a></sup>
Fission reactors generally need <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fissile_material" title="Fissile material">fissile</a> <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_uranium" title="Isotopes of uranium">isotope of uranium</a>.
The concentration of uranium-235 in natural uranium is very low (about 0.7%). Some reactors can use this natural uranium as fuel, depending on their <a href="/wiki/Neutron_economy" title="Neutron economy">neutron economy</a>. These reactors generally have graphite or <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> moderators.
For light water reactors, the most common type of reactor, this concentration is too low, and it must be increased by a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">uranium enrichment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc_fuel_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_fuel-59">[59]</a></sup> In civilian light water reactors, uranium is typically enriched to 3.5–5% uranium-235.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna_fuel_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna_fuel-60">[60]</a></sup>
The uranium is then generally converted into <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxide</a> (UO<sub>2</sub>), a ceramic, that is then compressively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sintered" title="Sintered">sintered</a> into fuel pellets, a stack of which forms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rod" title="Fuel rod">fuel rods</a> of the proper composition and geometry for the particular reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna_fuel_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna_fuel-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>After some time in the reactor, the fuel will have reduced fissile material and increased fission products, until its use becomes impractical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna_fuel_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna_fuel-60">[60]</a></sup> At this point, the spent fuel will be moved to a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> which provides cooling for the thermal heat and shielding for ionizing radiation. After several months or years, the spent fuel is radioactively and thermally cool enough to be moved to dry storage casks or reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna_fuel_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-wna_fuel-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uranium_resources">Uranium resources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">Uranium market</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">Uranium mining</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Energy_development#Nuclear" title="Energy development">Energy development § Nuclear</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions_(horizontal).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg/440px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg/660px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg/880px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions_%28horizontal%29.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions_(horizontal).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proportions of the isotopes <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (blue) and uranium-235 (red) found in natural uranium and in <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> for different applications. Light water reactors use 3–5% enriched uranium, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactors work with natural uranium.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> is a fairly common <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in the Earth's crust: it is approximately as common as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, and is about 40 times more common than <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
Uranium is present in trace concentrations in most rocks, dirt, and ocean water, but is generally economically extracted only where it is present in high concentrations.
Uranium mining can be underground, <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open-pit</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> mining. An increasing number of the highest output mines are remote underground operations, such as <a href="/wiki/McArthur_River_uranium_mine" title="McArthur River uranium mine">McArthur River uranium mine</a>, in Canada, which by itself accounts for 13% of global production.
As of 2011 the world's known resources of uranium, economically recoverable at the arbitrary price ceiling of US$130/kg, were enough to last for between 70 and 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Red_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Red-64">[64]</a></sup>
In 2007, the OECD estimated 670 years of economically recoverable uranium in total conventional resources and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> ores assuming the then-current use rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Light water reactors make relatively inefficient use of nuclear fuel, mostly using only the very rare uranium-235 isotope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a> can make this waste reusable, and newer reactors also achieve a more efficient use of the available resources than older ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-66">[66]</a></sup> With a pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> fuel cycle with a burn up of all the uranium and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> (which presently make up the most hazardous substances in nuclear waste), there is an estimated 160,000 years worth of uranium in total conventional resources and phosphate ore at the price of 60–100 US$/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> However, reprocessing is expensive, possibly dangerous and can be used to manufacture nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repr_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-repr-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-future1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-future1-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civlib_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-civlib-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-detect_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-detect-72">[72]</a></sup> One analysis found that for uranium prices could increase by two orders of magnitudes between 2035 and 2100 and that there could be a shortage near the end of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> A 2017 study by researchers from MIT and <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">WHOI</a> found that "at the current consumption rate, global conventional reserves of terrestrial uranium (approximately 7.6 million tonnes) could be depleted in a little over a century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Limited uranium-235 supply may inhibit substantial expansion with the current nuclear technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-sol1-75">[75]</a></sup> While various ways to reduce dependence on such resources are being explored,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> new nuclear technologies are considered to not be available in time for climate change mitigation purposes or competition with alternatives of renewables in addition to being more expensive and require costly research and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-sol1-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil1_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-mil1-80">[80]</a></sup> A study found it to be uncertain whether identified resources will be developed quickly enough to provide uninterrupted fuel supply to expanded nuclear facilities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and various forms of mining may be challenged by ecological barriers, costs, and land requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Researchers also report considerable import dependence of nuclear energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2018.12.024_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2018.12.024-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.anucene.2017.08.019_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.anucene.2017.08.019-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1002/ente.201600444_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1002/ente.201600444-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unconventional uranium resources also exist. Uranium is naturally present in seawater at a concentration of about 3 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> per liter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.ie_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.ie-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> with 4.4 billion tons of uranium considered present in seawater at any time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-91">[91]</a></sup>
In 2014 it was suggested that it would be economically competitive to produce nuclear fuel from seawater if the process was implemented at large scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
Like fossil fuels, over geological timescales, uranium extracted on an industrial scale from seawater would be replenished by both river erosion of rocks and the natural process of uranium <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(metallurgy)" title="Leaching (metallurgy)">dissolved</a> from the surface area of the ocean floor, both of which maintain the <a href="/wiki/Solubility_equilibrium" title="Solubility equilibrium">solubility equilibria</a> of seawater concentration at a stable level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-91">[91]</a></sup>
Some commentators have argued that this strengthens the case for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">nuclear power to be considered a renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste">Waste</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">Nuclear waste</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/330px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/495px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/660px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical composition of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> fuel before and after approximately three years in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Once-through_nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Once-through nuclear fuel cycle">once-through nuclear fuel cycle</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWR" title="LWR">LWR</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaif_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaif-94">[94]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The normal operation of nuclear power plants and facilities produce <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a>, or nuclear waste. This type of waste is also produced during plant decommissioning. There are two broad categories of nuclear waste: low-level waste and high-level waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc_waste_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_waste-95">[95]</a></sup> The first has low radioactivity and includes contaminated items such as clothing, which poses limited threat. High-level waste is mainly the spent fuel from nuclear reactors, which is very radioactive and must be cooled and then safely disposed of or reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc_waste_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_waste-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level_waste">High-level waste</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/High-level_waste" title="High-level waste">High-level waste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">Spent nuclear fuel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="976" data-file-width="1304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/220px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/330px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/440px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Activity of spent UOx fuel in comparison to the activity of natural <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ore</a> over time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m.phys.org_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-m.phys.org-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaif_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-jaif-94">[94]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1139" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/220px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/330px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/440px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">Dry cask storage</a> vessels storing spent nuclear fuel assemblies</div></div></div>
<p>The most important waste stream from nuclear power reactors is <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>, which is considered <a href="/wiki/High-level_waste" title="High-level waste">high-level waste</a>. For LWRs, spent fuel is typically composed of 95% uranium, 4% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a>, and about 1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic" title="Transuranic">transuranic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinides" title="Actinides">actinides</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The fission products are responsible for the bulk of the short-term radioactivity, whereas the plutonium and other transuranics are responsible for the bulk of the long-term radioactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>High-level waste (HLW) must be stored isolated from the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> with sufficient shielding so as to limit radiation exposure.  After being removed from the reactors, used fuel bundles are stored for six to ten years in <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pools</a>, which provide cooling and shielding against radiation. After that, the fuel is cool enough that it can be safely transferred to <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The radioactivity decreases exponentially with time, such that it will have decreased by 99.5% after 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The more intensely radioactive short-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">fission products</a> (SLFPs) decay into stable elements in approximately 300 years, and after about 100,000 years, the spent fuel becomes less radioactive than natural uranium ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaif_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-jaif-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commonly suggested methods to isolate LLFP waste from the biosphere include separation and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaif_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-jaif-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synroc" title="Synroc">synroc</a> treatments, or deep geological storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thermal-neutron_reactor" title="Thermal-neutron reactor">Thermal-neutron reactors</a>, which presently constitute the majority of the world fleet, cannot burn up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a> that is generated during the reactor operation. This limits the life of nuclear fuel to a few years.
In some countries, such as the United States, spent fuel is classified in its entirety as a nuclear waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
In other countries, such as France, it is largely reprocessed to produce a partially recycled fuel, known as mixed oxide fuel or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOX" title="MOX">MOX</a>. For spent fuel that does not undergo reprocessing, the most concerning isotopes are the medium-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic elements</a>, which are led by reactor-grade plutonium (half-life 24,000 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
Some proposed reactor designs, such as the <a href="/wiki/Integral_fast_reactor" title="Integral fast reactor">integral fast reactor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">molten salt reactors</a>, can use as fuel the plutonium and other actinides in spent fuel from light water reactors, thanks to their <a href="/wiki/Fast_fission" title="Fast fission">fast fission</a> spectrum. This offers a potentially more attractive alternative to deep geological disposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a> results in similar fission products, though creates a much smaller proportion of transuranic elements from <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> events within a reactor. Spent thorium fuel, although more difficult to handle than spent uranium fuel, may present somewhat lower proliferation risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level_waste">Low-level waste</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low-level_waste" title="Low-level waste">Low-level waste</a></div>
<p>The nuclear industry also produces a large volume of <a href="/wiki/Low-level_waste" title="Low-level waste">low-level waste</a>, with low radioactivity, in the form of contaminated items like clothing, hand tools, water purifier resins, and (upon decommissioning) the materials of which the reactor itself is built. Low-level waste can be stored on-site until radiation levels are low enough to be disposed of as ordinary waste, or it can be sent to a low-level waste disposal site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_relative_to_other_types">Waste relative to other types</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste#Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material" title="Radioactive waste">Radioactive waste § Naturally occurring radioactive material</a></div>
<p>In countries with nuclear power, radioactive wastes account for less than 1% of total industrial toxic wastes, much of which remains hazardous for long periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-66">[66]</a></sup> Overall, nuclear power produces far less waste material by volume than fossil-fuel based power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Coal-burning plants, in particular, produce large amounts of toxic and mildly radioactive ash resulting from the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material" title="Naturally occurring radioactive material">naturally occurring radioactive materials</a> in coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
A 2008 report from <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> concluded that coal power actually results in more radioactivity being released into the environment than nuclear power operation, and that the population <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effective_dose_equivalent" title="Effective dose equivalent">effective dose equivalent</a> from radiation from coal plants is 100 times that from the operation of nuclear plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-colmain_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-colmain-115">[115]</a></sup>
Although coal ash is much less radioactive than spent nuclear fuel by weight, coal ash is produced in much higher quantities per unit of energy generated. It is also released directly into the environment as <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, whereas nuclear plants use shielding to protect the environment from radioactive materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cejournal_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-cejournal-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear waste volume is small compared to the energy produced. For example, at <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a>, which generated 44 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> of electricity when in service, its complete spent fuel inventory is contained within sixteen casks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> It is estimated that to produce a lifetime supply of energy for a person at a western <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> (approximately 3<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GWh" title="GWh">GWh</a>) would require on the order of the volume of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_can" title="Soda can">soda can</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" title="Low enriched uranium">low enriched uranium</a>, resulting in a similar volume of spent fuel generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generation_Atomic_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Generation_Atomic-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr.org_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr.org-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_radioactive_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of radioactive waste treatment technologies">List of radioactive waste treatment technologies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg"><img alt="Storage of radioactive waste at WIPP" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg/220px-WIPP-04.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_flask" title="Nuclear flask">Nuclear waste flasks</a> generated by the United States during the Cold War are stored underground at the <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> (WIPP) in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>. The facility is seen as a potential demonstration for storing spent fuel from civilian reactors.</div></div></div>
<p>Following interim storage in a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a>, the bundles of used fuel rod assemblies of a typical nuclear power station are often stored on site in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
Presently, waste is mainly stored at individual reactor sites and there are over 430 locations around the world where radioactive material continues to accumulate.
</p><p>Disposal of nuclear waste is often considered the most politically divisive aspect in the lifecycle of a nuclear power facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-122">[122]</a></sup>
With the lack of movement of nuclear waste in the 2 billion year old <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> being cited as "a source of essential information today."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
Experts suggest that centralized underground repositories which are well-managed, guarded, and monitored, would be a vast improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-122">[122]</a></sup>
There is an "international consensus on the advisability of storing nuclear waste in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repositories</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-go-125">[125]</a></sup> With the advent of new technologies, other methods including <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_drillhole_disposal" title="Horizontal drillhole disposal">horizontal drillhole disposal</a> into geologically inactive areas have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/220px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/330px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/440px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most waste packaging, small-scale experimental fuel recycling chemistry and <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmaceutical" title="Radiopharmaceutical">radiopharmaceutical</a> refinement is conducted within remote-handled <a href="/wiki/Hot_cell" title="Hot cell">hot cells</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>There are no commercial scale purpose built underground high-level waste repositories in operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-go-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> However, in Finland the <a href="/wiki/Onkalo_spent_nuclear_fuel_repository" title="Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository">Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant">Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant</a> is under construction as of 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprocessing">Reprocessing</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement" title="Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement">Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement</a></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Thermal-neutron_reactor" title="Thermal-neutron reactor">thermal-neutron reactors</a> run on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Once-through_nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Once-through nuclear fuel cycle">once-through nuclear fuel cycle</a>, mainly due to the low price of fresh uranium.
However, many reactors are also fueled with recycled fissionable materials that remain in spent nuclear fuel. The most common fissionable material that is recycled is the <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">reactor-grade plutonium</a> (RGPu) that is extracted from spent fuel, it is mixed with uranium oxide and fabricated into mixed-oxide or <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">MOX fuel</a>.
Because thermal LWRs remain the most common reactor worldwide, this type of recycling is the most common. It is considered to increase the sustainability of the nuclear fuel cycle, reduce the attractiveness of spent fuel to theft, and lower the volume of high level nuclear waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
Spent MOX fuel cannot generally be recycled for use in thermal-neutron reactors. This issue does not affect <a href="/wiki/Fast-neutron_reactor" title="Fast-neutron reactor">fast-neutron reactors</a>, which are therefore preferred in order to achieve the full energy potential of the original uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_Spectrum_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_Spectrum-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main constituent of spent fuel from LWRs is slightly <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a>. This can be recycled into <a href="/wiki/Reprocessed_uranium" title="Reprocessed uranium">reprocessed uranium</a> (RepU), which can be used in a fast reactor, used directly as fuel in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactors, or re-enriched for another cycle through an LWR.
Re-enriching of reprocessed uranium is common in France and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA3_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA3-134">[134]</a></sup>
Reprocessed uranium is also safer in terms of nuclear proliferation potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reprocessing has the potential to recover up to 95% of the uranium and plutonium fuel in spent nuclear fuel, as well as reduce long-term radioactivity within the remaining waste.
However, reprocessing has been politically controversial because of the potential for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a> and varied perceptions of increasing the vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas2011_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-138">[138]</a></sup> Reprocessing also leads to higher fuel cost compared to the once-through fuel cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas2011_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-138">[138]</a></sup>
While reprocessing reduces the volume of high-level waste, it does not reduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> that are the primary causes of residual heat generation and radioactivity for the first few centuries outside the reactor. Thus, reprocessed waste still requires an almost identical treatment for the initial first few hundred years.
</p><p>Reprocessing of civilian fuel from power reactors is currently done in France, the United Kingdom, Russia, Japan, and India. In the United States, spent nuclear fuel is currently not reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA3_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA3-134">[134]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/La_Hague_site" title="La Hague site">La Hague reprocessing facility</a> in France has operated commercially since 1976 and is responsible for half the world's reprocessing as of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> It produces MOX fuel from spent fuel derived from several countries. More than 32,000 tonnes of spent fuel had been reprocessed as of 2015, with the majority from France, 17% from Germany, and 9% from Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear-Fuel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1250" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nuclear-Fuel.jpg/170px-Nuclear-Fuel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nuclear-Fuel.jpg/255px-Nuclear-Fuel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nuclear-Fuel.jpg/340px-Nuclear-Fuel.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear-Fuel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">Nuclear fuel</a> assemblies being inspected before entering a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> in the United States</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a></div>
<p>Breeding is the process of converting non-fissile material into fissile material that can be used as nuclear fuel. The non-fissile material that can be used for this process is called <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">fertile material</a>, and constitute the vast majority of current nuclear waste.
This breeding process occurs naturally in <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeder reactors</a>. As opposed to light water thermal-neutron reactors, which use uranium-235 (0.7% of all natural uranium), fast-neutron breeder reactors use uranium-238 (99.3% of all natural uranium) or thorium.
A number of fuel cycles and breeder reactor combinations are considered to be sustainable or renewable sources of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brundtland_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brundtland-142">[142]</a></sup> In 2006 it was estimated that with seawater extraction, there was likely five billion years' worth of uranium resources for use in breeder reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford-cohen_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-cohen-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Breeder technology has been used in several reactors, but as of 2006, the high cost of reprocessing fuel safely requires uranium prices of more than US$200/kg before becoming justified economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-anpr_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-anpr-144">[144]</a></sup>
Breeder reactors are however being developed for their potential to burn up all of the actinides (the most active and dangerous components) in the present inventory of nuclear waste, while also producing power and creating additional quantities of fuel for more reactors via the breeding process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
As of 2017, there are two breeders producing commercial power, <a href="/wiki/BN-600_reactor" title="BN-600 reactor">BN-600 reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800 reactor</a>, both in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-147">[147]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%A9nix" title="Phénix">Phénix</a> breeder reactor in France was powered down in 2009 after 36 years of operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-147">[147]</a></sup>
Both China and India are building breeder reactors. The Indian 500 MWe <a href="/wiki/Prototype_Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor">Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor</a> is in the commissioning phase,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> with plans to build more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another alternative to fast-neutron breeders are thermal-neutron breeder reactors that use uranium-233 bred from <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> as fission fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-150">[150]</a></sup> Thorium is about 3.5 times more common than uranium in the Earth's crust, and has different geographic characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India's three-stage nuclear power programme">India's three-stage nuclear power programme</a> features the use of a thorium fuel cycle in the third stage, as it has abundant thorium reserves but little uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decommissioning">Decommissioning</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">Nuclear decommissioning</a></div>
<p>Nuclear decommissioning is the process of dismantling a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_facility" title="Nuclear facility">nuclear facility</a> to the point that it no longer requires measures for radiation protection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> returning the facility and its parts to a safe enough level to be entrusted for other uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_decommissioning_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_decommissioning-152">[152]</a></sup>
Due to the presence of radioactive materials, nuclear decommissioning presents technical and economic challenges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
The costs of decommissioning are generally spread over the lifetime of a facility and saved in a decommissioning fund.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">Nuclear power by country</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:blue"></div>

<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:orange"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 93.906250581749px 100px 0; border-right-color:black"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:black"></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>2019 world electricity generation by source (total generation was 27 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petawatt-hour" title="Petawatt-hour">petawatt-hours</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Electricity_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Electricity-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:black; color:white;"> </span> Coal (37%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:orange; color:black;"> </span> Natural gas (24%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:blue; color:white;"> </span> Hydro (16%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:red; color:black;"> </span> Nuclear (10%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:green; color:white;"> </span> Wind (5%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:yellow; color:black;"> </span> Solar (3%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:white; color:black;"> </span> Other (5%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Civilian nuclear power supplied 2,586 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) of electricity in 2019, equivalent to about 10% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_electricity_generation" title="Global electricity generation">global electricity generation</a>, and was the second largest <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> source after <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pris-supplied_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-pris-supplied-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA2019_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA2019-158">[158]</a></sup>
Since electricity accounts for about 25% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">world energy consumption</a>, nuclear power's contribution to global energy was about 2.5% in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
This is a little more than the combined global electricity production from wind, solar, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and geothermal power, which together provided 2% of global final energy consumption in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
Nuclear power's share of global electricity production has fallen from 16.5% in 1997, in large part because the economics of nuclear power have become more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20180903_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20180903-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of March 2022,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">439 civilian fission reactors in the world</a>, with a combined electrical capacity of 392 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW). There are also 56 nuclear power reactors under construction and 96 reactors planned, with a combined capacity of 62<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW and 96<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-162">[162]</a></sup>
The United States has the largest fleet of nuclear reactors, generating over 800<span class="nowrap"> </span>TWh per year with an average <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> of 92%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-163">[163]</a></sup> Most reactors under construction are <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactors</a> in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regional differences in the use of nuclear power are large. The United States produces the most nuclear energy in the world, with nuclear power providing 20% of the electricity it consumes, while France produces the highest percentage of its electrical energy from nuclear reactors—71% in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-18">[18]</a></sup>
In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, nuclear power provides 26% of the electricity as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
Nuclear power is the single largest low-carbon electricity source in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-issues_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-issues-166">[166]</a></sup> and accounts for two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s low-carbon electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a> differs among European Union countries, and some, such as Austria, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Italy" title="Nuclear power in Italy">Italy</a>, have no active nuclear power stations.
</p><p>In addition, there were approximately 140 naval vessels using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a> in operation, powered by about 180 reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
These include military and some civilian ships, such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear-powered_icebreaker" title="Nuclear-powered icebreaker">nuclear-powered icebreakers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>International research is continuing into additional uses of process heat such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">hydrogen production</a> (in support of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>), for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalinating</a> sea water, and for use in <a href="/wiki/District_heating" title="District heating">district heating</a> systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of nuclear power plants">Economics of nuclear power plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_in_the_nuclear_sector" title="List of companies in the nuclear sector">List of companies in the nuclear sector</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">cost of electricity by source</a></div>
<p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject and multi-billion-dollar investments depend on the choice of energy sources. Nuclear power plants typically have high capital costs for building the plant. For this reason, comparison with other power generation methods is strongly dependent on assumptions about construction timescales and capital financing for nuclear plants. Fuel costs account for about 30 percent of the operating costs, while prices are subject to the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnmoney_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnmoney-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The high cost of construction is one of the biggest challenges for nuclear power plants. A new 1,100<span class="nowrap"> </span>MW plant is estimated to cost between $6 billion to $9 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Nuclear power cost trends show large disparity by nation, design, build rate and the establishment of familiarity in expertise. The only two nations for which data is available that saw cost decreases in the 2000s were India and South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovering2016_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovering2016-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must also take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties.
As of 2010, all operating nuclear power plants have been developed by state-owned or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">electric utility</a> monopolies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-175">[175]</a></sup>
Many countries have since liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_electricity" title="Levelized cost of electricity">levelized cost of electricity</a> (LCOE) from a new nuclear power plant is estimated to be 69<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh, according to an analysis by the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> and the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">Nuclear Energy Agency</a>. This represents the median cost estimate for an nth-of-a-kind nuclear power plant to be completed in 2025, at a <a href="/wiki/Discounting" title="Discounting">discount rate</a> of 7%. Nuclear power was found to be the least-cost option among <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable technologies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_LCOE_2020_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_LCOE_2020-177">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">Variable renewables</a> can generate cheaper electricity: the median cost of onshore wind power was estimated to be 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh, and utility-scale solar power 56<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_LCOE_2020_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_LCOE_2020-177">[177]</a></sup>
At the assumed CO<sub>2</sub> emission cost of 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/ton, power from coal (88<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh) and gas (71<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh) is more expensive than low-carbon technologies.
Electricity from long-term operation of nuclear power plants by lifetime extension was found the be the least-cost option, at 32<span class="nowrap"> </span>USD/MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_LCOE_2020_177-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_LCOE_2020-177">[177]</a></sup> 
Measures to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate global warming</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" title="Carbon emissions trading">carbon emissions trading</a>, may favor the economics of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Extreme weather events, including events made more severe by climate change, are decreasing all energy source reliability including nuclear energy by a small degree, depending on location siting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>New <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_modular_reactors" title="Small modular reactors">small modular reactors</a>, such as those developed by <a href="/wiki/NuScale_Power" title="NuScale Power">NuScale Power</a>, are aimed at reducing the investment costs for new construction by making the reactors smaller and modular, so that they can be built in a factory.
</p><p>Certain designs had considerable early positive economics, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a>, which realized much higher <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> and reliability when compared to generation II light water reactors up to the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power plants, though capable of some grid-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Load_following" title="Load following">load following</a>, are typically run as much as possible to keep the cost of the generated electrical energy as low as possible, supplying mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base-load" title="Base-load">base-load</a> electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
Due to the on-line refueling reactor design, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PHWR" title="PHWR">PHWRs</a> (of which the CANDU design is a part) continue to hold many world record positions for longest continual electricity generation, often over 800 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
The specific record as of 2019 is held by a PHWR at <a href="/wiki/Kaiga_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Kaiga Atomic Power Station">Kaiga Atomic Power Station</a>, generating electricity continuously for 962 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Costs not considered in LCOE calculations include funds for research and development, and disasters (the Fukushima disaster is estimated to cost taxpayers ≈$187 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian-20170130_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20170130-186">[186]</a></sup>). Governments were found to in some cases force "consumers to pay upfront for potential cost overruns"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil1_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-mil1-80">[80]</a></sup> or subsidize uneconomic nuclear energy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> or be required to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-francere_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-francere-52">[52]</a></sup> Nuclear operators are liable to pay for the waste management in the EU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euwastecosts_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-euwastecosts-188">[188]</a></sup> In the U.S. the Congress reportedly decided 40 years ago that the nation, and not private companies, would be responsible for storing radioactive waste with taxpayers paying for the costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The World Nuclear Waste Report 2019 found that "even in countries in which the polluter-pays-principle is a legal requirement, it is applied incompletely" and notes the case of the German <a href="/wiki/Asse_II_mine" title="Asse II mine">Asse II deep geological disposal facility</a>, where the retrieval of large amounts of waste has to be paid for by taxpayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Similarly, other forms of energy, including fossil fuels and renewables, have a portion of their costs covered by governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_space">Use in space</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="327" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg/220px-Msl-MMRTG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Multi-mission_radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator">multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (MMRTG), used in several space missions such as the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> Mars rover</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_space" title="Nuclear power in space">Nuclear power in space</a></div>
<p>The most common use of nuclear power in space is the use of <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>, which use <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> to generate power.
These power generators are relatively small scale (few kW), and they are mostly used to power <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_mission" title="Space mission">space missions</a> and experiments for long periods where solar power is not available in sufficient quantity, such as in the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> space probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_space_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA_space-192">[192]</a></sup>
A few space vehicles have been launched using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>: 34 reactors belong to the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RORSAT" title="RORSAT">RORSAT</a> series and one was the American <a href="/wiki/SNAP-10A" title="SNAP-10A">SNAP-10A</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_space_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA_space-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> and fusion appear promising for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">space propulsion</a> applications, generating higher mission velocities with less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_space_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-WNA_space-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_and_security" title="Nuclear safety and security">Nuclear safety and security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_safety_system" title="Nuclear reactor safety system">Nuclear reactor safety system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_(including_solar).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg/220px-Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg/330px-Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg/440px-Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_%28including_solar%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Death_rates_from_energy_production_per_TWh_(including_solar).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Death rates from air pollution and accidents related to energy production, measured in deaths in the past per terawatt hours (TWh)</div></div></div>
<p>Nuclear power plants have three unique characteristics that affect their safety, as compared to other power plants.
Firstly, intensely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_material" title="Radioactive material">radioactive materials</a> are present in a nuclear reactor. Their release to the environment could be hazardous.
Secondly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a>, which make up most of the intensely radioactive substances in the reactor, continue to generate a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> even after the fission <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">chain reaction</a> has stopped. If the heat cannot be removed from the reactor, the fuel rods may overheat and release radioactive materials.
Thirdly, a <a href="/wiki/Criticality_accident" title="Criticality accident">criticality accident</a> (a rapid increase of the reactor power) is possible in certain reactor designs if the chain reaction cannot be controlled.
These three characteristics have to be taken into account when designing nuclear reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAsafety_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAsafety-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>All modern reactors are designed so that an uncontrolled increase of the reactor power is prevented by natural feedback mechanisms, a concept known as negative <a href="/wiki/Void_coefficient" title="Void coefficient">void coefficient</a> of reactivity. If the temperature or the amount of steam in the reactor increases, the fission rate inherently decreases. The chain reaction can also be manually stopped by inserting <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> into the reactor core. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_core_cooling_system" title="Emergency core cooling system">Emergency core cooling systems</a> (ECCS) can remove the decay heat from the reactor if normal cooling systems fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> If the ECCS fails, multiple physical barriers limit the release of radioactive materials to the environment even in the case of an accident. The last physical barrier is the large <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAsafety_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAsafety-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>With a death rate of 0.07 per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TWh" title="TWh">TWh</a>, nuclear power is the safest energy source per unit of energy generated in terms of mortality when the historical track-record is considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
Energy produced by coal, petroleum, natural gas and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> has caused more deaths per unit of energy generated due to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>.
This is found when comparing the immediate deaths from other energy sources to both the immediate and the latent, or predicted, indirect cancer deaths from nuclear energy accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theage2006_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-198">[198]</a></sup>
