When the direct and indirect fatalities (including fatalities resulting from the mining and air pollution) from nuclear power and fossil fuels are compared,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-199">[199]</a></sup> the use of nuclear power has been calculated to have prevented about 1.84 million deaths from air pollution between 1971 and 2009, by reducing the proportion of energy that would otherwise have been generated by fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895-201">[201]</a></sup>
Following the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, it has been estimated that if Japan had never adopted nuclear power, accidents and pollution from coal or gas plants would have caused more lost years of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Serious impacts of nuclear accidents are often not directly attributable to radiation exposure, but rather social and psychological effects. Evacuation and long-term displacement of affected populations created problems for many people, especially the elderly and hospital patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
Forced evacuation from a nuclear accident may lead to social isolation, anxiety, depression, psychosomatic medical problems, reckless behavior, and suicide.
A comprehensive 2005 study on the aftermath of the Chernobyl disaster concluded that the mental health impact is the largest public health problem caused by the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riv12_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-riv12-204">[204]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Frank_N._von_Hippel" title="Frank N. von Hippel">Frank N. von Hippel</a>, an American scientist, commented that a disproportionate fear of ionizing radiation (<a href="/wiki/Radiophobia" title="Radiophobia">radiophobia</a>) could have long-term psychological effects on the population of contaminated areas following the Fukushima disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27–36_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27–36-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents">Accidents</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="1095" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/220px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/330px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/440px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following the 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, the world's worst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a> since 1986, 50,000 households were displaced after <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans of some shipments of vegetables and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="784" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/220px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/330px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/440px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reactor <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> as a fraction of full power after the reactor shutdown, using two different correlations. To remove the decay heat, reactors need cooling after the shutdown of the fission reactions. A loss of the ability to remove decay heat caused the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_accident" title="Fukushima accident">Fukushima accident</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">Energy accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_nuclear_disasters_and_radioactive_incidents" title="Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents">Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents</a></div>
<p>Some serious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have occurred. The severity of nuclear accidents is generally classified using the <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">International Nuclear Event Scale</a> (INES) introduced by the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA).
The scale ranks anomalous events or accidents on a scale from 0 (a deviation from normal operation that poses no safety risk) to 7 (a major accident with widespread effects).
There have been three accidents of level 5 or higher in the civilian nuclear power industry, two of which, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_accident" title="Fukushima accident">Fukushima accident</a>, are ranked at level 7.
</p><p>The first major nuclear accidents were the <a href="/wiki/Kyshtym_disaster" title="Kyshtym disaster">Kyshtym disaster</a> in the Soviet Union and the <a href="/wiki/Windscale_fire" title="Windscale fire">Windscale fire</a> in the United Kingdom, both in 1957.  The first major accident at a nuclear reactor in the USA occurred in 1961 at the <a href="/wiki/SL-1" title="SL-1">SL-1</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a> experimental nuclear power reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>. An uncontrolled chain reaction resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a> which killed the three crew members and caused a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">meltdown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ido19313_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-ido19313-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
Another serious accident happened in 1968, when one of the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid-metal-cooled_reactor" title="Liquid-metal-cooled reactor">liquid-metal-cooled reactors</a> on board the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">Soviet submarine <i>K-27</i></a> underwent a <a href="/wiki/Fuel_element_failure" title="Fuel element failure">fuel element failure</a>, with the emission of gaseous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> into the surrounding air, resulting in 9 crew fatalities and 83 injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident was caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Tohoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami</a>.
The accident has not caused any radiation-related deaths but resulted in radioactive contamination of surrounding areas. The difficult <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_disaster_cleanup" title="Fukushima disaster cleanup">cleanup operation</a> is expected to cost tens of billions of dollars over 40 or more years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Schiffman_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Schiffman-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Fackler_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Fackler-212">[212]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979 was a smaller scale accident, rated at INES level 5.
There were no direct or indirect deaths caused by the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-213">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>The impact of nuclear accidents is controversial. According to <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_K._Sovacool" title="Benjamin K. Sovacool">Benjamin K. Sovacool</a>, fission <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a> ranked first among energy sources in terms of their total economic cost, accounting for 41 percent of all property damage attributed to energy accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Another analysis found that coal, oil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_petroleum_gas" title="Liquid petroleum gas">liquid petroleum gas</a> and hydroelectric accidents (primarily due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam_disaster" title="Banqiao Dam disaster">Banqiao Dam disaster</a>) have resulted in greater economic impacts than nuclear power accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> The study compares latent cancer deaths attributable to nuclear with immediate deaths from other energy sources per unit of energy generated, and does not include fossil fuel related cancer and other indirect deaths created by the use of fossil fuel consumption in its "severe accident" (an accident with more than five fatalities) classification. The Chernobyl accident in 1986 caused approximately 50 deaths from direct and indirect effects, and some temporary serious injuries from <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">acute radiation syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-216">[216]</a></sup> The future predicted mortality from increases in cancer rates is estimated at about 4000 in the decades to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_2008_D_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_2008_D-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> However, the costs have been large and are increasing.
</p><p>Nuclear power works under an <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a> framework that limits or structures accident liabilities in accordance with national and international conventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>
It is often argued that this potential shortfall in liability represents an external cost not included in the cost of nuclear electricity. This cost is small, amounting to about 0.1% of the <a href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_electricity" title="Levelized cost of electricity">levelized cost of electricity</a>, according to a study by the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">Congressional Budget Office</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>
These beyond-regular insurance costs for worst-case scenarios are not unique to nuclear power. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">Hydroelectric power</a> plants are similarly not fully insured against a catastrophic event such as <a href="/wiki/Dam_failure" title="Dam failure">dam failures</a>. For example, the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam" title="Banqiao Dam">Banqiao Dam</a> caused the death of an estimated 30,000 to 200,000 people, and 11 million people lost their homes. As private insurers base dam insurance premiums on limited scenarios, major disaster insurance in this sector is likewise provided by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_and_sabotage">Attacks and sabotage</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack" title="Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack">Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">Nuclear terrorism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear safety in the United States">Nuclear safety in the United States</a></div>
<p>Terrorists could target <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a> in an attempt to release <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> into the community. The United States 9/11 Commission has said that nuclear power plants were potential targets originally considered for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>. An attack on a reactor's <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> could also be serious, as these pools are less protected than the reactor core. The release of radioactivity could lead to thousands of near-term deaths and greater numbers of long-term fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the NRC carries out "Force on Force" (FOF) exercises at all nuclear power plant sites at least once every three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-223">[223]</a></sup>
In the United States, plants are surrounded by a double row of tall fences which are electronically monitored.
The plant grounds are patrolled by a sizeable force of armed guards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insider sabotage is also a threat because insiders can observe and work around security measures.
Successful insider crimes depended on the perpetrators' observation and knowledge of security vulnerabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>
A fire caused 5–10 million dollars worth of damage to New York's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Energy Center</a> in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
The arsonist was a plant maintenance worker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proliferation">Proliferation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement" title="Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement">Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/330px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/440px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United States and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>/Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> stockpiles, 1945–2006. The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a> was the main driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usec.com_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-usec.com-229">[229]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_(CG_61)_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_(FAS)_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_(CVN_73).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_%28CG_61%29_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_%28FAS%29_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_%28CVN_73%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_%28CG_61%29_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_%28FAS%29_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_%28CVN_73%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_%28CG_61%29_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_%28FAS%29_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_%28CVN_73%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_(CG_61)_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_(FAS)_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_(CVN_73).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The guided-missile cruiser USS Monterey (CG 61) receives fuel at sea (FAS) from the Nimitz-class aircraft carrier USS George Washington (CVN 73).</div></div></div>
<p>Nuclear proliferation is the spread of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, fissionable material, and weapons-related nuclear technology to states that do not already possess nuclear weapons. Many technologies and materials associated with the creation of a nuclear power program have a dual-use capability, in that they can also be used to make nuclear weapons. For this reason, nuclear power presents proliferation risks.
</p><p>Nuclear power program can become a route leading to a nuclear weapon. An example of this is the concern over <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran" title="Nuclear program of Iran">Iran's nuclear program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-230">[230]</a></sup>
The re-purposing of civilian nuclear industries for military purposes would be a breach of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation_treaty" title="Non-proliferation treaty">Non-Proliferation Treaty</a>, to which 190 countries adhere.
As of April 2012, there are <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">thirty one countries</a> that have civil nuclear power plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> of which <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">nine have nuclear weapons</a>. The vast majority of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_state" title="Nuclear weapons state">nuclear weapons states</a> have produced weapons before commercial nuclear power stations.
</p><p>A fundamental goal for global security is to minimize the nuclear proliferation risks associated with the expansion of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-230">[230]</a></sup>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Nuclear_Energy_Partnership" title="Global Nuclear Energy Partnership">Global Nuclear Energy Partnership</a> was an international effort to create a distribution network in which developing countries in need of energy would receive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> at a discounted rate, in exchange for that nation agreeing to forgo their own indigenous development of a uranium enrichment program.
The France-based <a href="/wiki/Eurodif" title="Eurodif">Eurodif</a>/<i>European Gaseous Diffusion Uranium Enrichment Consortium</i> is a program that successfully implemented this concept, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Spain" title="Nuclear power in Spain">Spain</a> and other countries without enrichment facilities buying a share of the fuel produced at the French-controlled enrichment facility, but without a transfer of technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
Iran was an early participant from 1974 and remains a shareholder of Eurodif via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sofidif" title="Sofidif">Sofidif</a>.
</p><p>A 2009 United Nations report said that:
</p>
<blockquote><p>the revival of interest in nuclear power could result in the worldwide dissemination of uranium enrichment and spent fuel reprocessing technologies, which present obvious risks of proliferation as these technologies can produce fissile materials that are directly usable in nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bks2011_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-233">[233]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, power reactors can also reduce nuclear weapons arsenals when military-grade nuclear materials are reprocessed to be used as fuel in nuclear power plants.
The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a> is considered the single most successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation" title="Non-proliferation">non-proliferation</a> program to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-228">[228]</a></sup>
Up to 2005, the program had processed $8 billion of high enriched, weapons-grade uranium into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" title="Low enriched uranium">low enriched uranium</a> suitable as nuclear fuel for commercial fission reactors by diluting it with <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a>.
This corresponds to the elimination of 10,000 nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup>
For approximately two decades, this material generated nearly 10 percent of all the electricity consumed in the United States, or about half of all U.S. nuclear electricity, with a total of around 7,000<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TWh" title="TWh">TWh</a> of electricity produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-235">[235]</a></sup>
In total it is estimated to have cost $17 billion, a "bargain for US ratepayers", with Russia profiting $12 billion from the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-235">[235]</a></sup> Much needed profit for the Russian nuclear oversight industry, which after the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_economy" title="Soviet economy">Soviet economy</a>, had difficulties paying for the maintenance and security of the Russian Federations highly enriched uranium and warheads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-236">[236]</a></sup>
The Megatons to Megawatts Program was hailed as a major success by anti-nuclear weapon advocates as it has largely been the driving force behind the sharp reduction in the number of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_228-2"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-228">[228]</a></sup>
However, without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for fissile fuel, the cost of dismantling and down blending has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.
As of 2013 Russia appears to not be interested in extending the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact<span class="anchor" id="Environmental_issues"></span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/220px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/330px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/440px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ikata_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ikata Nuclear Power Plant">Ikata Nuclear Power Plant</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> that cools by utilizing a secondary coolant <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchanger</a> with a large body of water, an alternative cooling approach to large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a></div></div></div>
<p>Being a low-carbon energy source with relatively little land-use requirements, nuclear energy can have a positive environmental impact. It also requires a constant supply of significant amounts of water and affects the environment through mining and milling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.3390/ijerph13070700_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.3390/ijerph13070700-241">[241]</a></sup> Its largest potential negative impacts on the environment may arise from its transgenerational risks for nuclear weapons proliferation that may increase risks of their use in the future, risks for problems associated with the management of the radioactive waste such as groundwater contamination, risks for accidents and for risks for various forms of attacks on waste storage sites or reprocessing- and power-plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repr_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-repr-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi1_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-wi1-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldnuclearwastereport_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldnuclearwastereport-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-risks_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-risks-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plane1_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-plane1-245">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.3390/ijerph13070700_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.3390/ijerph13070700-241">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dont_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-dont-247">[247]</a></sup> However, these remain mostly only risks as historically there have only been few disasters at nuclear power plants with known relatively substantial environmental impacts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_emissions">Carbon emissions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse_gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions of energy sources">Life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions of energy sources</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="#Historic_effect_on_carbon_emissions">§ Historic effect on carbon emissions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="1244" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/220px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/330px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/440px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions of electricity supply technologies, median values calculated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-248">[248]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Nuclear power is one of the leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> methods of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, and in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">total life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions per unit of energy generated</a>, has emission values comparable to or lower than <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nrel.gov_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nrel.gov-249">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>
A 2014 analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> literature by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) reported that the embodied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">total life-cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> of nuclear power has a median value of 12<span class="nowrap"> </span>g CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">eq</a>/<a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a>, which is the lowest among all commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-251">[251]</a></sup>
This is contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> at 820 and 490 g CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> eq/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-251">[251]</a></sup>
As of 2021, nuclear reactors worldwide have helped avoid the emission of 72 billion tonnes of carbon dioxide since 1970, compared to coal-fired electricity generation, according to a report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation">Radiation</span></h3>
<p>The average dose from natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a> is 2.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">millisievert</a> per year (mSv/a) globally. It varies between 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a and 13<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a, depending mostly on the geology of the location. According to the United Nations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" title="UNSCEAR">UNSCEAR</a>), regular nuclear power plant operations, including the nuclear fuel cycle, increases this amount by 0.0002<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a of public exposure as a global average. The average dose from operating nuclear power plants to the local populations around them is less than 0.0001<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-253">[253]</a></sup>
For comparison, the average dose to those living within 50 miles of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_power_plant" title="Coal power plant">coal power plant</a> is over three times this dose, at 0.0003<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chernobyl resulted in the most affected surrounding populations and male recovery personnel receiving an average initial 50 to 100<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv over a few hours to weeks, while the remaining global legacy of the worst nuclear power plant accident in average exposure is 0.002<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv/a and is continuously dropping at the decaying rate, from the initial high of 0.04<span class="nowrap"> </span>mSv per person averaged over the entire populace of the Northern Hemisphere in the year of the accident in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-253">[253]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate">Debate</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-nuclear_movement" title="Pro-nuclear movement">Pro-nuclear movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Anti-nuclear movement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2478" data-file-width="2879" decoding="async" height="379" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png/440px-3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png/660px-3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png/880px-3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3-Learning-curves-for-electricity-prices.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of prices over time for energy from nuclear fission and from other sources. Over the presented time, thousands of wind turbines and similar were built on assembly lines in mass production resulting in an economy of scale. While nuclear remains bespoke, many first of their kind facilities added in the timeframe indicated and none are in serial production.  <i>Our World in Data</i> notes that this cost is the global <i>average</i>, while the 2 projects that drove nuclear pricing upwards were in the US. The organization recognises that the <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> cost of the most exported and produced nuclear energy facility in the 2010s the South Korean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/APR1400" title="APR1400">APR1400</a>, remained "constant", including in export.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><br/><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_energy" title="Levelized cost of energy">LCOE</a> is a measure of the average net present cost of electricity generation for a generating plant over its lifetime. As a metric, it remains controversial as the lifespan of units are not independent but manufacturer projections, not a demonstrated longevity.</small></div></div></div>
<p>The nuclear power debate concerns the controversy which has surrounded the deployment and use of nuclear fission reactors to generate electricity from nuclear fuel for civilian purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Proponents of nuclear energy regard it as a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> and increases <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> by decreasing dependence on other energy sources that are also<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2018.12.024_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2018.12.024-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.anucene.2017.08.019_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.anucene.2017.08.019-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1002/ente.201600444_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1002/ente.201600444-87">[87]</a></sup> often dependent on imports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-257">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> For example, proponents note that annually, nuclear-generated electricity reduces 470 million metric tons of carbon dioxide emissions that would otherwise come from fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> Additionally, the amount of comparatively low waste that nuclear energy does create is safely disposed of by the large scale nuclear energy production facilities or it is repurposed/recycled for other energy uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a>, who popularized the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, saw oil as a resource that would run out and considered nuclear energy its replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup>
Proponents also claim that the present quantity of nuclear waste is small and can be reduced through the latest technology of newer reactors and that the operational safety record of fission-electricity in terms of deaths is so far "unparalleled".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernard_L._Cohen_1990_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_L._Cohen_1990-14">[14]</a></sup> Kharecha and <a href="/wiki/James_Hansen" title="James Hansen">Hansen</a> estimated that "global nuclear power has prevented an average of 1.84 million air pollution-related deaths and 64 gigatonnes of CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent (GtCO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub>-eq) greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions that would have resulted from fossil fuel burning" and, if continued, it could prevent up to 7 million deaths and 240<span class="nowrap"> </span>GtCO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub>-eq emissions by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895_201-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>Proponents also bring to attention the opportunity cost of utilizing other forms of electricity. For example, the Environmental Protection Agency estimates that coal kills 30,000 people a year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> as a result of its environmental impact, while 60 people died in the Chernobyl disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> A real world example of impact provided by proponents is the 650,000 ton increase in carbon emissions in the two months following the closure of the Vermont Yankee nuclear plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>Opponents believe that nuclear power poses many threats to people's health and environment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturgis_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-267">[267]</a></sup> such as the risk of nuclear weapons proliferation, long-term safe waste management and terrorism in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gierec_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-gierec-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protest_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-protest-269">[269]</a></sup> They also contend that nuclear power plants are complex systems where many things can and have gone wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bksenpol_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-bksenpol-270">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup> Costs of the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> amount to ≈$68 billion as of 2019 and are increasing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-34">[34]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima disaster</a> is estimated to cost taxpayers ~$187 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian-20170130_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20170130-186">[186]</a></sup> and radioactive waste management is estimated to cost the EU nuclear operators ~$250 billion by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euwastecosts_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-euwastecosts-188">[188]</a></sup> However, in countries that already use nuclear energy, when not considering reprocessing, intermediate nuclear waste disposal costs could be relatively fixed to certain but unknown degrees<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> "as the main part of these costs stems from the operation of the intermediate storage facility".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics find that one of the largest drawbacks to building new nuclear fission power plants are the large construction and operating costs when compared to alternatives of sustainable energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnchina_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnnchina-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015-274">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1177/2399654418777765_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1177/2399654418777765-275">[275]</a></sup>
Further costs include costs for ongoing research and development, expensive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a> in cases where such is practiced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repr_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-repr-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-future1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-future1-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-pluto-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-detect_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-detect-72">[72]</a></sup> and decommissioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.rser.2021.110836_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.rser.2021.110836-278">[278]</a></sup> Proponents note that focussing on the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Levelized_Cost_of_Energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Levelized Cost of Energy (page does not exist)">Levelized Cost of Energy</a> (LCOE), however, ignores the value premium associated with 24/7 dispatchable electricity and the cost of storage and backup systems necessary to integrate variable energy sources into a reliable electrical grid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> "Nuclear thus remains the dispatchable low-carbon technology with the lowest expected costs in 2025. Only large hydro reservoirs can provide a similar contribution at comparable costs but remain highly dependent on the natural endowments of individual countries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg/220px-Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg/330px-Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg/440px-Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%BCne_protests_against_nuclear_energy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-nuclear protest near <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">nuclear waste disposal centre</a> at <a href="/wiki/Gorleben" title="Gorleben">Gorleben</a> in northern Germany</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, many opponents find that nuclear energy cannot meaningfully contribute to climate change mitigation. In general, they find it to be, too dangerous, too expensive, to take too long for deployment, to be an obstacle to achieving a transition towards sustainability and carbon-neutrality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slowexpensive_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-slowexpensive-282">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> effectively being a distracting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gates2_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-gates2-284">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cd1_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-cd1-285">[285]</a></sup> competition for resources (i.e. human, financial, time, infrastructure and expertise) for the deployment and development of alternative, sustainable, <a href="/wiki/Energy_system" title="Energy system">energy system</a> technologies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil1_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-mil1-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cd1_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-cd1-285">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> (such as for wind, ocean and solar<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup> – including e.g. <a href="/wiki/Floating_solar" title="Floating solar">floating solar</a> – as well as ways to manage <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">their intermittency</a> other than nuclear baseload<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT2018_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT2018-287">[287]</a></sup> generation such as <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable generation</a>, renewables-diversification,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.esr.2019.01.007_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.esr.2019.01.007-289">[289]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Super_grid" title="Super grid">super grids</a>, flexible energy demand and supply regulating <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">smart grids</a> and energy storage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-294">[294]</a></sup> technologies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.04.013_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.04.013-295">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dont_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-dont-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, there is ongoing research and debate over costs of new nuclear, especially in regions where i.a. seasonal energy storage is difficult to provide and which aim to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">phase out fossil fuels</a> in favor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power" title="Low carbon power">low carbon power</a> faster than the global average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup> Some find that financial transition costs for a 100% renewables-based European energy system that has completely phased out nuclear energy could be more costly by 2050 based on current technologies (i.e. not considering potential advances in e.g. <a href="/wiki/Green_hydrogen" title="Green hydrogen">green hydrogen</a>, transmission and flexibility capacities, ways to reduce energy needs, geothermal energy and fusion energy) when the grid only extends across Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> Arguments of economics and safety are used by both sides of the debate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_renewable_energy">Comparison with renewable energy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a></div>
<p>Slowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> requires a transition to a <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_economy" title="Low-carbon economy">low-carbon economy</a>, mainly by burning far less <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>. Limiting global warming to 1.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C is technically possible if no new fossil fuel power plants are built from 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> This has generated considerable interest and dispute in determining the best path forward to rapidly replace fossil-based fuels in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_energy_consumption" title="Global energy consumption">global energy mix</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> with intense academic debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM-NewFront_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-NewFront-309">[309]</a></sup> Sometimes the IEA says that countries without nuclear should develop it as well as their renewable power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px">
<div class="mw-no-invert" style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black;transform:scaleX(-1)rotate(-90deg)">
<div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#00CC4B"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 880.42099845207px; border-left-color:#005CE6"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#005CE6"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#005CE6"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 361.07774271147px; border-left-color:#de2821"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#de2821"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#de2821"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 153.28270598844px; border-left-color:#ABFF57"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#ABFF57"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#ABFF57"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 41.789897502735px; border-left-color:#ef8e39"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#ef8e39"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#ef8e39"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:54.112125212688px 84.094458229817px 0 0; border-top-color:#313c42"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#313c42"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 47.056428121225px 100px 0; border-right-color:#7C6250"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>World total primary energy supply of 162,494 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hour#Watt_hour_multiples_and_billing_units" title="Kilowatt hour">TWh</a> (or 13,792 <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a>) by fuels in 2017 (IEA, 2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2019_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2019-311">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6, 8">: 6, 8 </span></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#7C6250; color:white;"> </span> Oil (32%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#313c42; color:white;"> </span> Coal/Peat/Shale (27.1%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef8e39; color:black;"> </span> Natural Gas (22.2%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ABFF57; color:black;"> </span> Biofuels and waste (9.5%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#de2821; color:white;"> </span> Nuclear (4.9%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#005CE6; color:white;"> </span> Hydro (2.5%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00CC4B; color:black;"> </span> Others (<a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewables</a>) (1.8%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Several studies suggest that it might be theoretically possible to cover a majority of world energy generation with new renewable sources.
The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has said that if governments were supportive, renewable energy supply could account for close to 80% of the world's energy use by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipccccc_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-312">[312]</a></sup>
While in developed nations the economically feasible geography for new hydropower is lacking, with every geographically suitable area largely already exploited,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> some proponents of wind and solar energy claim these resources alone could eliminate the need for nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM-NewFront_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-NewFront-309">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power is comparable to, and in some cases lower, than many renewable energy sources in terms of lives lost in the past per unit of electricity delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starfelt_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starfelt-315">[315]</a></sup> Depending on recycling of renewable energy technologies, nuclear reactors may produce a much smaller volume of waste, although much more toxic, expensive to manage and longer-lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldnuclearwastereport_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldnuclearwastereport-243">[243]</a></sup> A nuclear plant also needs to be disassembled and removed and much of the disassembled nuclear plant needs to be stored as low-level nuclear waste for a few decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup> The disposal and management of the wide variety<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup> of radioactive waste, of which there are over one quarter of a million tons as of 2018, can cause future damage and costs across the world <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste#Fuel_composition_and_long_term_radioactivity" title="Radioactive waste">for over or during hundreds of thousands of years</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup> – possibly over a million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spektr_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-spektr-322">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> due to issues such as leakage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup> malign retrieval, vulnerability to attacks (including of reprocessing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civlib_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-civlib-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repr_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-repr-68">[68]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack" title="Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack">power plants</a>), groundwater contamination, radiation and leakage to above ground, brine leakage or bacterial corrosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spektr_322-1"><a href="#cite_note-spektr-322">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statusreport_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-statusreport-329">[329]</a></sup> The European Commission Joint Research Centre found that as of 2021 the necessary technologies for geological disposal of nuclear waste are now available and can be deployed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup> Corrosion experts noted in 2020 that putting the problem of storage off any longer "isn't good for anyone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup> Separated <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> could be used for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, which – even with the current centralized control (e.g. state-level) and level of prevalence – are considered to be a difficult and <a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk#Warfare_and_mass_destruction" title="Global catastrophic risk">substantial global risk</a> for substantial future impacts on human health, lives, civilization and the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repr_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-repr-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi1_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-wi1-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldnuclearwastereport_243-2"><a href="#cite_note-worldnuclearwastereport-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-risks_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-risks-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plane1_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-plane1-245">[245]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_of_transition_and_investment_needed">Speed of transition and investment needed</span></h4>
<p>Analysis in 2015 by professor <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and colleagues found that nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely within 10 years. This finding was based on the historically modest and proven rate at which nuclear energy was added in France and Sweden during their building programs in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals.plos.org_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-journals.plos.org-332">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup> In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, including transportation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuels" title="Synthetic fuels">synthetic fuels</a> etc., could be generated within approximately 30 years if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to historical proven installation rates calculated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year per unit of global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> (GW/year/$).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook_could_2012_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-brook_could_2012-334">[334]</a></sup>
This is in contrast to the conceptual studies for <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> systems, which would require an order of magnitude more costly global investment per year, which has no historical precedent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loftus_critical_2015_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-loftus_critical_2015-335">[335]</a></sup> These renewable scenarios would also need far greater land devoted to onshore wind and onshore solar projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook_could_2012_334-1"><a href="#cite_note-brook_could_2012-334">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loftus_critical_2015_335-1"><a href="#cite_note-loftus_critical_2015-335">[335]</a></sup> Brook notes that the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook_could_2012_334-2"><a href="#cite_note-brook_could_2012-334">[334]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientific data indicates that – assuming 2021 emissions levels – humanity only has a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_budget" title="Carbon budget">carbon budget</a> equivalent to 11 years of emissions left for limiting warming to 1.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup> while the construction of new nuclear reactors took a median of 7.2–10.9 years in 2018–2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statusreport_329-1"><a href="#cite_note-statusreport-329">[329]</a></sup> substantially longer than, alongside other measures, scaling up the deployment of wind and solar – especially for novel reactor types – as well as being more risky, often delayed and more dependent on state-support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slowexpensive_282-1"><a href="#cite_note-slowexpensive-282">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gates2_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-gates2-284">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldnuclearreport_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldnuclearreport-340">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.04.013_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.04.013-295">[295]</a></sup> Researchers have cautioned that novel nuclear technologies – which have been in development since decades,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-6"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015-274">[274]</a></sup> are less tested, have higher <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste#Proliferation_concerns" title="Radioactive waste">proliferation risks</a>, have more new safety problems, are often far from commercialization and are more expensive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015_274-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.erss.2014.04.015-274">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.5281/zenodo.5573718_79-7"><a href="#cite_note-10.5281/zenodo.5573718-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012_240-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.enpol.2016.03.012-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adva1_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-adva1-342">[342]</a></sup> – are not available in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol1_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-sol1-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil1_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-mil1-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gates2_284-2"><a href="#cite_note-gates2-284">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1080/00963402.2021.1941600_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1080/00963402.2021.1941600-344">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-294">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup> Critics of nuclear energy often only oppose nuclear fission energy but not nuclear fusion; however, fusion energy is unlikely to be commercially widespread before 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-346">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fusion2_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-fusion2-347">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fusiongua_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-fusiongua-348">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fusion3_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-fusion3-349">[349]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fusion4_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-fusion4-350">[350]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span></h4>
<p>The median land area used by US nuclear power stations per 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>GW installed capacity is 1.3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_miles" title="Square miles">square miles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEI_news_2015_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEI_news_2015-351">[351]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_gov_Fast_Facts_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_gov_Fast_Facts-352">[352]</a></sup> To generate the same amount of electricity annually (taking into account <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factors</a>) from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_PV" title="Solar PV">solar PV</a> would require about 60 square miles, and from a wind farm about 310 square miles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEI_news_2015_351-1"><a href="#cite_note-NEI_news_2015-351">[351]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_gov_Fast_Facts_352-1"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_gov_Fast_Facts-352">[352]</a></sup> Not included in this is land required for the associated transmission lines, water supply, rail lines, mining and processing of nuclear fuel, and for waste disposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_fission_reactor_designs">Advanced fission reactor designs</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactor</a></div>
<p>Current fission reactors in operation around the world are <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">second</a> or <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">third generation</a> systems, with most of the first-generation systems having been already retired.
Research into advanced <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">generation IV reactor</a> types was officially started by the Generation IV International Forum (GIF) based on eight technology goals, including to improve economics, safety, proliferation resistance, natural resource utilization and the ability to consume existing nuclear waste in the production of electricity.
Most of these reactors differ significantly from current operating light water reactors, and are expected to be available for commercial construction after 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[354]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_fusion-fission">Hybrid fusion-fission</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion%E2%80%93fission_hybrid" title="Nuclear fusion–fission hybrid">Nuclear fusion–fission hybrid</a></div>
<p>Hybrid nuclear power is a proposed means of generating power by the use of a combination of nuclear fusion and fission processes. The concept dates to the 1950s and was briefly advocated by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to delays in the realization of pure fusion. When a sustained nuclear fusion power plant is built, it has the potential to be capable of extracting all the fission energy that remains in spent fission fuel, reducing the volume of nuclear waste by orders of magnitude, and more importantly, eliminating all actinides present in the spent fuel, substances which cause security concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-355">[355]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fusion">Fusion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2040" data-file-width="2040" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/220px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/330px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/440px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of the <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> under construction in France</div></div></div>
